  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer4049826577575865847"><div><b>From "a striking new talent"(Sandra Dallas, author of <i>Tallgrass</i>) comes an unforgettable debut novel of young love and coming of age in an Iran headed toward revolution.</b><br/><br/> In this poignant, eye-opening and emotionally vivid novel, Mahbod Seraji lays bare the beauty and brutality of the centuries-old Persian culture, while reaffirming the human experiences we all share.<br/><br/> In a m</div></span>
  <span id="freeText4049826577575865847" style="display:none"><div><b>From "a striking new talent"(Sandra Dallas, author of <i>Tallgrass</i>) comes an unforgettable debut novel of young love and coming of age in an Iran headed toward revolution.</b><br /><br /> In this poignant, eye-opening and emotionally vivid novel, Mahbod Seraji lays bare the beauty and brutality of the centuries-old Persian culture, while reaffirming the human experiences we all share.<br /><br /> In a middle-class neighborhood of Iran's sprawling capital city, 17-year-old Pasha Shahed spends the summer of 1973 on his rooftop with his best friend Ahmed, joking around one minute and asking burning questions about life the next. He also hides a secret love for his beautiful neighbor Zari, who has been betrothed since birth to another man. But the bliss of Pasha and Zari's stolen time together is shattered when Pasha unwittingly acts as a beacon for the Shah's secret police. The violent consequences awaken him to the reality of living under a powerful despot, and lead Zari to make a shocking choice...</div></span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1169754-what-ages-of-young-readers-would-this-book">What ages of young readers would this book be appropriate for?? I teach 7th graders and I have heard great things it, unfortunately I have a HUGE stack of books I need to read to decide if I can include them in my classroom library and there are just not enough hours in a day to read them all. From the reviews I have read it sounds like a lovely book and I hope to read it soon. Please respond. Thank you in advance.</a>
</div>

<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/67047018-cavak"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_100x100-6433b08cbe793211f55e184d39e84c4d.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/67047018-cavak">Cavak</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Seventh graders can read this; its messages of political oppression, culture, and death are equal to other books often presented at that level.<br/><br/>I think<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Seventh graders can read this; its messages of political oppression, culture, and death are equal to other books often presented at that level.<br /><br />I think what may raise concerns are the romances, some of the crude jokes, and the loss of faith. It might be hard to talk about them in a classroom setting, or there may be a lack of empathy for Pasha during the second half of the book. Might lead to some interesting discussions.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

    There are two Masked Angels :) The first one is Zari's cousin who comes to stay with the family for a short period of time. The second is Zari, after <a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    There are two Masked Angels :) The first one is Zari's cousin who comes to stay with the family for a short period of time. The second is Zari, after faking her death, due to circumstances described in the book.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1871959337?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" href="/user/show/31461418-alienor-french-frowner">Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1871959337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4068341510760964326"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1484866343i/21762365._SX540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>~ Full review ~</b> (EDIT : Translated Books List's link)<br/><br/><b>
  <u>3.5 stars</u>
</b>. When I first realized that <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6001011.Rooftops_of_Tehran' title='Rooftops of Tehran by Mahbod Seraji' rel='nofollow noopener'>Rooftops of Tehran</a> was very much a romance and not the political novel I thought it would be, I was surprised - but curious. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485020290i/21778045._SX540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/> <br/>The truth is, I've never read a romance novel set in Iran. Ever. More than that, <b>as a French woman, the only things I do know about Iran - and it's not much - are painted in a negative light.</b> And I'm gonna say it : <br/><br/>That <br/>shit <br/>is <br/>dangerous. <br/><br/>Thinking in the lines of Bad Countries™ </span>
  <span id="freeText4068341510760964326" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1484866343i/21762365._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>~ Full review ~</b> (EDIT : Translated Books List's link)<br><br><b>
  <u>3.5 stars</u>
</b>. When I first realized that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6001011.Rooftops_of_Tehran" title="Rooftops of Tehran by Mahbod Seraji" rel="nofollow noopener">Rooftops of Tehran</a> was very much a romance and not the political novel I thought it would be, I was surprised - but curious. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485020290i/21778045._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br>The truth is, I've never read a romance novel set in Iran. Ever. More than that, <b>as a French woman, the only things I do know about Iran - and it's not much - are painted in a negative light.</b> And I'm gonna say it : <br><br>That <br>shit <br>is <br>dangerous. <br><br>Thinking in the lines of Bad Countries™ versus Good Countries™ is fucking dangerous - because tell me now, if we judge a country - its PEOPLE - on behalf of its leaders' faults, first we're flirting with Manicheism like real morons and secondly... well I guess we're fucking hypocrites? <br><br>So, tell me, why does a romance novel - even flawed - is important? Well, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2747995.Mahbod_Seraji" title="Mahbod Seraji" rel="nofollow noopener">Mahbod Seraji</a>'s words in his author note say it better than I ever would : <br><blockquote>
  <b>"At a time when the country of my birth is often portrayed in the news media as "the enemy," I chose to tell a story about friendship and humor, love and hope, universal experiences valued by people in all times and places. I wanted to show a side of Iran that's usually hidden from view - its warm, funny, generous people."</b>
</blockquote><br>A day like today, I really think we should sit one second and think about that - even when talking about a far from perfect novel. Because this book? No matter how annoyed I could be at some point, how some details - and more important parts - could have been handled better in my opinion, <u>this novel reinforced this sentiment</u> : guys, I'm sorry if it's not obvious to you and I don't want to offense anyone, but <b>I am SICK of the way the whole book community is US/UK/AUS centered, including when it comes to diversity</b> (don't get me wrong though, reading about and from minorities living in the US is much NEEDED and important and I am willing to read more and more diverse books, we can - need to - do BOTH). <br><br>Because, <i>guys!</i> There is a <b>WHOLE WORLD OUT THERE.</b> There are THOUSANDS of writers who don't write in English, because some people, even writers, *GASP* <i>don't speak English</i>. MIND. BLOWN. And they are very rarely translated because.... well I guess because it doesn't interest Americans enough? I don't know? <b>I kinda think now would be the time to open up and start looking elsewhere, don't you?</b> <br><br>I've been thinking more and more about this lately... and <b>I'm building up a blog right now.</b> I can't dive into the details yet, just know that I'll focus on translated books from all over the world. By the way, if you have recommendations for me, please use the comment section! I'll welcome them :) <b>(I created a list so feel free to add books there : <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/list/show/107908.Translated_Books_Project">https://www.goodreads.com/list/show/1...</a>)</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485020290i/21778046._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br>As I said earlier, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6001011.Rooftops_of_Tehran" title="Rooftops of Tehran by Mahbod Seraji" rel="nofollow noopener">Rooftops of Tehran</a> is very much a romance.... yet firmly anchored in the real world, that is to say, 1970s Iran. I found fascinating to follow the characters' struggle because it <b>gave me an insight</b> that otherwise I would not have gotten. When dealing with a different country, especially in the Middle-East/West Asia, we Western countries often focus on their leaders, while here on the contrary the story primarily brought the random citizens' life to light. This way, we the readers are able to see how the politics influence every part of the everyday of the inhabitants. I loved that because it felt way more real.<br> <br><b>A warning though</b> : as I said, it takes place in 1970s Iran. Parts of the narrative brought up by some characters - and if not condoned by the mc, not strongly rejected either - would make me reluctant to recommend it to members or the LGBTQIA community, because if sadly realistic at this period of time, they could still be triggering. <br><br>All in all, I was satisfied by the way political matters were tackled, even if I wish some issues wouldn't have been so IN YOUR FACE. Yet even if the way the US was put on a bit of a pedestal annoyed me at some point, I very much appreciated that their role in helping the Shah keep the power was mentioned. <br><br>More than anything else in our world, <b>we need nuances.</b> I know that 2016 taught us that the loudest people would win the day, but we need to resist the urge to express our feelings in snap judgments. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485020290i/21778047._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br>As much as I enjoyed following these characters, I can't deny that <b>they sometimes lacked complexity, especially the secondary characters.</b> Indeed if Pasha and Ahmed's friendship was everything (<b>friendship GOAL</b> here), the dynamics between the other characters were too superficial for me to get fully involved. Zari especially made it hard for me to understand her : her decisions - especially near the end - seemed too sudden and all over the place, and I had a hard time suspending my disbelief. She didn't quite make sense to me? In the same way, the romance was cute but I never really felt 100% involved in it. <br><br>As for the <b>writing,</b> some of the dialogues seemed like more lectures than anything else, and I can't deny that it felt like infodump<i>-ish</i> Iran 101 too. Good thing I had never taken that curse, I suppose, but someone educated on Iran's history might have found it rather tiring. Yet <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6001011.Rooftops_of_Tehran" title="Rooftops of Tehran by Mahbod Seraji" rel="nofollow noopener">Rooftops of Tehran</a> was very compelling : albeit simple, and not out-of-world amazing, the writing made the novel <b>extremely readable.</b> <br><br>Am I more lenient because of the settings? Maybe I am. Is it really a big deal if I am? I don't think so? Look, <b>I read so many uninspired romance novels taking place in the US, I sure enjoyed this one <i>more</i>.</b> <br><br>Let's see what you think, shall we?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485020290i/21778048.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>For more of my reviews, please visit:</i><br><blockquote>
  <a href="https://meetthebookworld.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487851828i/22057164._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="4068341510760964326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1008430941" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1008430941" />
    <a title="Margitte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte"><img alt="Margitte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361772481p2/7481492.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1008430941?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margitte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margitte" href="/user/show/7481492-margitte">Margitte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=family-sagas">family-sagas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=iran">iran</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=biographical-fiction">biographical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=middle-eastern">middle-eastern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=community">community</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=2017-read">2017-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=drama">drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1008430941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer941417232212689940">I acquired this book a long time ago and it got lost somewhere in my stacks of books. I was elated when I found it this morning, after trying to find it for many months, and decided to spend the entire Sunday in bed, reading a tale I marked boldly <b>MUST READ</b> on my TBR list in my diary in 2012. <br/><br/>Colorful, descriptive, detailed, compassionate, entertaining, rich in color and culture, is how I would love to summarize this fictional biography which is mainly based on the author's memories of his life </span>
  <span id="freeText941417232212689940" style="display:none">I acquired this book a long time ago and it got lost somewhere in my stacks of books. I was elated when I found it this morning, after trying to find it for many months, and decided to spend the entire Sunday in bed, reading a tale I marked boldly <b>MUST READ</b> on my TBR list in my diary in 2012. <br /><br />Colorful, descriptive, detailed, compassionate, entertaining, rich in color and culture, is how I would love to summarize this fictional biography which is mainly based on the author's memories of his life in Iran, prior to living in America. <br /><br />He uses a fictional protagonist, Pasha Shahed who regarded life <i>"as a random series of beautifully composed vignettes, loosely tied together by a string of characters and time"</i> to act as himself in the narrative. <br /><br />Pasha had five friends: Ahmed, Faheemah, Iraj, Zari and Doctor(Ramin Sobhi, a political science major and political activist on the SAVAK hunt list).<br /><br />The young people discovered the value and truth behind, humor, love, hope and friendship in a tumultuous time in their history, while the reader accompanies them down the alleys of the neighborhood, spend nights with them on the roof, peek with them into the neighborhood windows, and fall in love with the girl next door. Warm, funny, generous people open up a culture that is normally hidden from the world.<br /><br />The story opens in 1974 with an old man standing somewhere in the buildings of the Roozbeh Psychiatric Hospital in Tehran, lost and disconnected from reality, chanting in an empty tone:<blockquote>If I had a book, I would read it.<br />If I had a song, I would sing it.<br />If I knew a dance, I would dance it.<br />If I knew a rhyme, I would chant it.<br />If I had a life, I would risk it.<br />If I could be free, I would chance it.<br />If I had a horse, I would ride it.<br />If I had a horse, I would ride it.<br />If I had a horse ...</blockquote>Seventeen-year-old Pasha Shahed finds himself(temporarily) in a wheelchair nearby with no memory of how he landed there. Slowly his memory returns through painful dreams ...<br /><br />A fictional tragic, but beautiful love story plays itself out amidst the revolt around the ousting of the Shah, in the 1970s in which a community have to deal with the secret police, and the devastation and torture resulting from the tyrannical ruler at the time.<br /><br />Most of the story plays out on the rooftops of Tehran and explains the title. It was so beautifully done that I felt like a family member running around with the young teenager in the alleys, playing chess or soccer on the street, challenging the rigid religious teachers, cry, laugh and love with a community who took care of each other. <br /><br />Four of the friends become involved in a tragic incident during a birthday celebration of the Shah of Iran, which will change their destinies forever. <br /><br />There's humor, tears, miracles and misery, and Ahmed's grandmother who was roaming the alleys looking for her husband who passed away a few years earlier. And Iraj who prefers to play chess against himself and then cannot decided who wins ... <br /><br />I have such a great admiration for Iran and its people. Old Persia with its ancient civilization and intriguing history have an ongoing story to tell to the world, and I appreciate the authors who are trying to introduce the real multicultural Iran to the world. Mahbod Seraji did a splendid job in <i>Rooftops of Iran</i> <br /><br />Last year we met an Iranian student doing research on leopards for his doctorate degree. He stayed with us for a few months, since my husband has been sponsoring leopard research for many many years. I was so surprised to learn that Iran has national parks, unbelievable wild life and one of the biggest leopard species in the world! By the time I met Benjamin, I already got to know more about this amazing, diverse country through the books I mentioned below. The beautiful warmhearted people inspired me to read more Iranian authors who might enrich my knowledge of a country I would love to visit one day. <br /><br />It is with this background that I wanted to read <i>Rooftops of Iran</i> - a heartfelt biographical fictional story with a strong autobiographical element. As such it is an excellent read since, IMHO, it does not really fall into the 'real' novel classification (such as <i>The Kite Runner</i> by Khaled Hosseini (Afghanistan story), although there is a little bit of suspense, lots of drama, and a well developed mystery to the tale with several colorful characters to fill up the heartbreaking, as well as heartwarming moments in the book.<br /><br />After closing the book, I wanted to read more about the author and discovered a audio version of the book on Youtube, a movie trailer and interesting interviews with the author.<br /><br />I'm pasting the links below.<br /><br />Movie trailer<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sci-cJ07NPY">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sci-c...</a><br /><br />Interview<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sFgzgtDBsPA">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sFgzg...</a><br /><br />A Goodreads interview:<br /> <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author_blog_posts/6703921-my-interview-with-new-asian-writiing">https://www.goodreads.com/author_blog...</a><br /><br />Audiobook<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=zgoaNvENuiA">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=zgoaN...</a><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8V_-FWjmnXA">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8V_-F...</a><br /><br />Two other memoirs which introduced me to this amazing country were:<br /><br />1) <i>
  <b>The Cypress Tree: A Love Letter to Iran</b>
</i> by Kamin Mohammadi<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/833315248">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> <br /><br />and<br /><br />2) <i>
  <b>A Beginner's Guide to Acting English</b>
</i> by Shappi Khorsandi <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/571167502">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><br />A WONDERFUL EXPERIENCE for historical fiction, as well as fictional biography readers who loves to explore the different cultures of the world. <br /><br />I loved it!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="941417232212689940" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_43899613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/43899613" />
    <a title="Tamera Davis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1941151-tamera-davis"><img alt="Tamera Davis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308373734p2/1941151.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/43899613?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tamera Davis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tamera Davis" href="/user/show/1941151-tamera-davis">Tamera Davis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer43899613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer807586535415895986">A friend in the media sent me an advance reader copy of this book. It swept me away. I'm still struggling to find my way back into my real world - yes, it's one of those books: the story stays with you, the characters don't fade; a beautiful touching story in the voice of a 17-year old, written like a pro, not a word out of place, not a thought out of character. So visually intense - I felt like I was watching a movie and not reading a book. Powerful, magnificently powerful. <br/>I was blown away by </span>
  <span id="freeText807586535415895986" style="display:none">A friend in the media sent me an advance reader copy of this book. It swept me away. I'm still struggling to find my way back into my real world - yes, it's one of those books: the story stays with you, the characters don't fade; a beautiful touching story in the voice of a 17-year old, written like a pro, not a word out of place, not a thought out of character. So visually intense - I felt like I was watching a movie and not reading a book. Powerful, magnificently powerful. <br />I was blown away by the intensity of the narrator's casual but sophisticated voice, his willingness to expose his vulnerabilities - reminding me of my first love (no I haven't forgotten you, if you happen to be reading) reminding me of the moment I lost my voice, my wits, and yes, my heart, too. <br />I didn't want the story to end - another 500 to 1000 pages would have sufficed! Have no doubt, Mr. Seraji, that soon everyone will be talking about ROOFTOPS OF TEHRAN. <br />just one question and you can send me the answer in a private email: Did all this really happen? </span>
  <a data-text-id="807586535415895986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1306464899" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1306464899" />
    <a title="Mansuriah Hassan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26126450-mansuriah-hassan"><img alt="Mansuriah Hassan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580314850p2/26126450.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1306464899?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mansuriah Hassan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mansuriah Hassan" href="/user/show/26126450-mansuriah-hassan">Mansuriah Hassan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26126450-mansuriah-hassan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1306464899" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10017751678421660821">This is such a lovely, lovely book. I fell in love with the story and the characters. It was far beyond what I could have imagined on any coming of age story to be. It had me on the edge of my chair alternately sobbing and then cheering for the characters. I have to call them characters to remind myself that this book is fiction. <br/><br/>The story begins in Iran in 1973 when the Shah is in power and his subjects are tightly overseen by his secret police. Life continues as in many countries as long as th</span>
  <span id="freeText10017751678421660821" style="display:none">This is such a lovely, lovely book. I fell in love with the story and the characters. It was far beyond what I could have imagined on any coming of age story to be. It had me on the edge of my chair alternately sobbing and then cheering for the characters. I have to call them characters to remind myself that this book is fiction. <br /><br />The story begins in Iran in 1973 when the Shah is in power and his subjects are tightly overseen by his secret police. Life continues as in many countries as long as their political beliefs are in line with that of the Shah or if they differ, they must be extremely well hidden from all.<br /><br />Seventeen year old Pasha Shahed is in his last year of high school and when he graduates, his father will send him to the United States to study engineering. He spends most of his free time with his friend Ahmed. Together, the boys spend hours atop Pasha's roof, which is common practise in Tehran. They talk of all those things that interest teenage boys: school, teachers, their families, futures and perhaps, most important of all, girls. Ahmed loves Faheemeh, a girl from a few streets away, while Pasha is in love with Zari, the girl next door who is engaged to another of his friend's.<br /><br />I love to read and learning about cultures that is different from mine. This was my first exposure to Iran outside of news reports. It was refreshing to learn of it's people and their daily life. They have many of the same concerns such as housing, food, education, but they must also contend with a government whose foremost concern is their leader. Any one who disagrees with the leader could be subject to dire consequences. This is something that Pasha must be ever conscious of.<br /><br />The author, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2747995.Mahbod_Seraji" title="Mahbod Seraji" rel="nofollow noopener">Mahbod Seraji</a>, breathed so much life into these characters that I guess that they will not be forgotten. The young love of Pasha and Zari had me goes head over heels. I felt compassion for Grandma and was awed that the entire neighbourhood looked after her. I could clearly picture the homes, alleyways, streets and rooftops. At times I laughed out loud, and at the book's climax it had me crying :P <br /><br />When you read it, you will find yourself wanting to go slowly, not wanting it to end :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="10017751678421660821" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_82137763" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82137763" />
    <a title="Derek Emerson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/638139-derek-emerson"><img alt="Derek Emerson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487118145p2/638139.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82137763?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Derek Emerson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Derek Emerson" href="/user/show/638139-derek-emerson">Derek Emerson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/638139-derek-emerson?shelf=2009-books-read">2009-books-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer82137763&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82137763" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8458798783013997136">Rooftops of Tehran offers us an important glimpse into Iran which most of us are not aware of in any real sense. Even now we are reading about the protests and killings happening because of many people's dissatisfaction with the ruling party. Rooftops also takes place during a time of frustration with the government, but this time it is with the Shah (which the U.S. put in power) and the outcome not seen in the book is the eventual overthrow of the government.<br/><br/>Here the time is 1973-74 and we foll</span>
  <span id="freeText8458798783013997136" style="display:none">Rooftops of Tehran offers us an important glimpse into Iran which most of us are not aware of in any real sense. Even now we are reading about the protests and killings happening because of many people's dissatisfaction with the ruling party. Rooftops also takes place during a time of frustration with the government, but this time it is with the Shah (which the U.S. put in power) and the outcome not seen in the book is the eventual overthrow of the government.<br /><br />Here the time is 1973-74 and we follow the life of four high school students, two boys and two girls, working on the transition to adulthood. Yes, a coming of age story, but with the secret police taking people without reason and erasing their existence, the decisions are a bit more important.<br /><br />The best part of this book for many non-Iranian readers will be the glimpse into the everyday world of Iran. Many of us think of the Iranian woman in a burka as the common standard. But here the burka is worn only by one extremely religious relative, the Masked Angel, in another city. Arranged marriages do exist, but the main one in this novel is broken in the face of true love (Ahmed and Faheemeh) and the love of Zari and Pasha (the two main characters) is welcomed by both sets of parents.<br /><br />The drama moves forward when Zari's "arranged boyfriend," Doctor, disappears after working as an activist against the government. SAVAK is the name of the secret police force which becomes a character in itself, always watching and controlling people even when they are not sure of when and how. We watch as Zari and the others deal with Doctor's disappearance, while Pasha deals with guilt of loving Zari despite her engagement and also because he is unintentionally responsible for SAVAK catching Doctor.<br /><br />SPOILER ALERT: Usually I avoid spoilers, but since my biggest frustration with the story comes in the plot direction, allow me to spoil! Doctor is killed in prison and the families are told to not mourn him. Zari is, of course, distraught over his death. However, it is clear throughout the book that she does not love him (she likes him) and that she loves Pasha. So it is surprising and hard to believe when she sets herself on fire on the 40th day of his death (a special time of mourning) while running into the Shah's birthday parade. Not only does she do this surprising act (she is hardly a radical), but she brings Pasha and their friends along to watch -- much to their horror. Pasha ends up in a mental institution (which we knew he would end up in since some chapters take place there, although until this incident we never know why he is there). Zari dies in her protest and Pasha returns home to find that the Masked Angel has moved into Zari's home (they are neighbors) to care for her parents. It takes one meeting with the Masked Angel and her whisper of a voice to figure out that Zari is alive and living in disguise. Why? Well, reasons are given, but none are easy to buy. SAVAK knows she is alive, so why hide it from anyone else? It takes Pasha a while to come to this realization and we wait not so patiently for this to develop. When it does she sends him off to the U.S. to study so he can return to her. <br /><br />This twist in the plot is unnecessary and way too "cute" to read without frustration. But the anti-climax of the revelation and the reunion of the "lovers" is also not worth the wait. This book makes a good story for the sappy romantics of the world, but they can find better stories than this to entertain them. I could be accused of a romantic leaning (I do love Jane Austen!), but this is too thick for me.<br /><br />What the book did encourage me to do is learn more about this country I still remember best for holding U.S. embassy employees hostage. I'm getting a non-fiction book on modern-day Iran so I can learn more of its recent history and current state. If Seraji's intent was to interest us in Iran, he is successful. It his intent was to write a great story, maybe the sequel will be better</span>
  <a data-text-id="8458798783013997136" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_482357246" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/482357246" />
    <a title="Regina Lindsey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6693237-regina-lindsey"><img alt="Regina Lindsey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348604150p2/6693237.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/482357246?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Regina Lindsey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Regina Lindsey" href="/user/show/6693237-regina-lindsey">Regina Lindsey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6693237-regina-lindsey?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6693237-regina-lindsey?shelf=shelfari-favorites">shelfari-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer482357246" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14802058452891338852">Every once in a while you stumble upon a book that, after reading it, you want to stop everyone on the street, hand them a copy, and insist they run home and read. Rooftops of Tehran is just that sort of book for me. <br/><br/> In many ways the themes are universal. It is a coming of age tale in which two life- long friends experience their first loves and shed the naivete that we all eventually must let go of when we realize life is not fair and is often cruel. But, set in Iran during the tumultuous 70’s</span>
  <span id="freeText14802058452891338852" style="display:none">Every once in a while you stumble upon a book that, after reading it, you want to stop everyone on the street, hand them a copy, and insist they run home and read. Rooftops of Tehran is just that sort of book for me. <br /><br /> In many ways the themes are universal. It is a coming of age tale in which two life- long friends experience their first loves and shed the naivete that we all eventually must let go of when we realize life is not fair and is often cruel. But, set in Iran during the tumultuous 70’s, the reader gets a glimpse into the history of Iran as well as its rich culture. In doing so, Seraji does his country a great service by bringing to light some of the lesser known aspects of the time. I’m always surprised to learn how many people are unaware of the U.S. role in the 1953 coup d'état. Further, so much of the writing regarding the 70’s focuses on the struggle between the Shah’s followers and the rising Islamic fundamentalists. Seraji demonstrates the danger those associated with Marxism faced. He brilliantly weaves the country’s history into the story by illustrating its impact on the psyche of the nation. “I wonder how we can explain this national impulse to cheat. Maybe it’s more a matter of sharing than cheating. I’ve heard that people in the West compete at everything, and that you’re either a loser or a winner. In my country, we don’t have that same competitive spirit. Centuries of misery under the dominance of the Moguls, Arabs, and internal despotic rulers have conditioned us to stick together and help each other through unpleasant situations.” Finally, he does the best job I’ve seen of illustrating the dynamics of US-Iranian relations at this time. I’ve read about the misconceptions that Iranians held during the era about Americans in many of the non-fiction books. Here, Seraji, illustrates them in ways that are quite humorous and brings clarity to a complicated subject. <br /><br />The book is incredibly well written. For me, character development is critical to make my favorites list. Here, the characters are so well drawn that you can’t help but experience every emotion alongside the residents of the alley, and, boy, is it an emotional rollercoaster. I laughed at the antics of Ahmed. I cheered on the boys as they routinely stepped into aid each other. I set aside the book twice to cry my eyes out. And, I was often angry, wishing Pasha would forget his promise to the brotherhood of the boxing fraternity and knock someone’s lights out. The alternating periods during the first part of the book creates an incredible tension in the book as the reader quickly falls in love with the characters but understands early on that something devastating has occurred. <br /><br />The only negative comment I can make is that the ending didn’t work for me. It simply wasn’t plausible. Because of that I should probably give it 4 stars, but I love the book so much I just can’t.<br /><br />I will never look at a red rose again without thinking of this book. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14802058452891338852" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_194507986" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/194507986" />
    <a title="Deb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5276322-deb"><img alt="Deb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526245087p2/5276322.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/194507986?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deb" href="/user/show/5276322-deb">Deb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer194507986" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4954240232492811044"><b>
  <i>Moving. Touching. Bittersweet. A story that attests to the unbreakable spirit.</i>
</b><br/><br/>I truly and thoroughly enjoyed every inch of this book. I commend the writer, <i>Mahbod Seraji</i> who himself talks in the <i>Readers Guide</i> (yes, it was that good I read into the readers guide) about the struggle to learn English when he came to America from Iran, for the beauty and brilliantly colorful way he weaves his words and presents to us this work of literary art. This is the story of Pasha Shahed, his undying love </span>
  <span id="freeText4954240232492811044" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>Moving. Touching. Bittersweet. A story that attests to the unbreakable spirit.</i>
</b><br /><br />I truly and thoroughly enjoyed every inch of this book. I commend the writer, <i>Mahbod Seraji</i> who himself talks in the <i>Readers Guide</i> (yes, it was that good I read into the readers guide) about the struggle to learn English when he came to America from Iran, for the beauty and brilliantly colorful way he weaves his words and presents to us this work of literary art. This is the story of Pasha Shahed, his undying love for Zari, his martyred mentor Doctor, his humorous best friend Ahmed and his fiancé Faheemah who all allow us a glimpse into their world seen from high atop the <b>
  <i>Rooftops of Tehran</i>
</b>. This is a story that winds itself inside your heart and finds a place to live there never to be forgotten. Touching, wrapping around, embracing you within the very world of these characters until you read the last page and you find yourself wanting more. A need spreads and grows. I want to know that they all are alright. I need to know that they've lived out their lives surviving hopefully better than what they all experienced over the short period that this book chronicled. This is not an autobiography, so says the author but the spirit of these characters is so masterfully harnessed and embodied within his words you know in some way, some how, elements of these people did, do, must have walked the earth. <br /><br />I've learned so much more than I knew about this country and this culture than I did before I read this book. Iran. As an American, I think I'm supposed to feel some type of way toward this country and it's people. As a person, a human being created by God I think, a country consists of a mass of people and how can I feel any type of way about a group of individuals I don't even know. Who don't know me. I don't know their individual stories and they don't know mine. I can't blind hate, it's not in me. Government stuff.. Well, I'm not very political and I leave that respectfully to those who know more. Me, I love to learn about cultures and people that I don't know about, because underneath all the blurred lines...we are all humans who live, love hard, experience hardship and tragedy, admire beauty, wish for peace and equality, to give our children the entire contents of our hearts and to die in peace. People at the crux of it all, are people. We may differ in shape, size, color or speech, even belief but at the center, in the pit and depth of a human, we're more the same than all the differences the small minded can list. That is what you will take away from this book. That you felt and deeply. Why? Because change the name, the place, circumstance, change it all.. What still stands, feelings. Emotions. This is the relation point. The connection. Any good book has it and this book has <i>"that"</i>. <br /><br />No doubt about it, this is one of those perfection 7 stars (GR5) books for me. I would have quoted more but just enjoying the story too much to stop and keep doing that when my available reading moments were precious to me. It's a great story, I want to recommend it to all but I don't think it's for all. It's full of love, it's full of heartache, it's tragic but it's also full of humor, culture and living. I love a book that draws me in emotional. If you're open to that, pick it up. If not, respectfully leave it on the shelf. I hate hearing books I love get ripped to shreds. I don't know yet if this author has more books but I will check them out. <br /><br />Not sure what will be my next read but the bar is definitely set high now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4954240232492811044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It is a story of hope,despite the abysmal darkness of a great loss. The voice evolves throughout the story, as does the young hero. Its piercing reality, relentless humor and often heartbreaking anguish turns the pages, and all along we hope we hope that love fills the void left by the loss--and we as readers are whole again.<br/>A touching tale that must be told </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7354503973676277333">This is a beautiful novel, with sensitively drawn characters in a volatile and violent period of Iran, from 1973 to 1974 during the reign of the Shah of Iran. At the heart of this novel is a love story, but there's much more. The sustaining nature of intimate friendships and family relationships, and the sacrifices that the characters make for each other are poetically and movingly portrayed. At a time when U.S. foreign policy with Iran weighs heavily, this novel provides a personal and valuable</span>
  <span id="freeText7354503973676277333" style="display:none">This is a beautiful novel, with sensitively drawn characters in a volatile and violent period of Iran, from 1973 to 1974 during the reign of the Shah of Iran. At the heart of this novel is a love story, but there's much more. The sustaining nature of intimate friendships and family relationships, and the sacrifices that the characters make for each other are poetically and movingly portrayed. At a time when U.S. foreign policy with Iran weighs heavily, this novel provides a personal and valuable insight into Iranian culture and history. <br /><br />We had the pleasure of hosting the author at the Hayward Public Library on Sept. 28, 2009, and I selected it for the December meeting of the Mostly Literary Fiction Book Group.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/187058032?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="April" class="user" itemprop="url" name="April" href="/user/show/833038-april">April</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer187058032" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer655118214538519189">I don't think I've ever openly cried for any characters from a book the way I did with this one. "Rooftops of Tehran" is a poignant book that absorbed me completely. I actually had to put it down for a bit as things became very intense when Zari's actions turn the tide in such a shocking way. I love Ahmed's character and that he is Pasha's best friend. I adore Pasha's emotions and thoughts on Zari. Even Pasha's father and his stories and the quirks of his mother endeared to me. I longed to be a </span>
  <span id="freeText655118214538519189" style="display:none">I don't think I've ever openly cried for any characters from a book the way I did with this one. "Rooftops of Tehran" is a poignant book that absorbed me completely. I actually had to put it down for a bit as things became very intense when Zari's actions turn the tide in such a shocking way. I love Ahmed's character and that he is Pasha's best friend. I adore Pasha's emotions and thoughts on Zari. Even Pasha's father and his stories and the quirks of his mother endeared to me. I longed to be a part of the dialogue that took place on the rooftops every night. I laughed when they laughed, cried at their losses, fumed at the injustices of their society--this was just an incredible read.<br /><br />Some beautiful, quotable moments for me include:<br /><br />"Faheemah is watching us, knowing full well that a seventeen-year-old boy has taken his first step toward becoming a man, and in the process has made her feel more like a woman than all the aunts, uncles, and formalities of the night before. If she must marry a man her parents have chosen for her, at least she knows that she is loved by someone with enough courage to defy tradition." <br /><br />"I wish I had the courage to tell them about myself and Zari, but love is a private matter, and the vault of the heart is not to be opened lightly, nor the treasure of love exposed. I always wondered why, despite our passionate spirit as a nation whose poetry is filled with declarations of love, our reality is one of guarded emotions when it comes to members of the opposite sex."<br /><br />"That's the way of the Persians--we are masters in the art of implication, sometimes at the cost of the point getting lost on an unsophisticated listener. Facts seldom matter. The meaning and the message are always woven into the fabric of our discourse. 'Deep in each knot of a Persian rug is a statement of the hands that patiently drove the needle and the thread,' I once heard my father say." <br /><br />"'I'd rather be in hell with you than without you in heaven....'"</span>
  <a data-text-id="655118214538519189" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer358542741" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4562517222089591150">I read Rooftops of Tehran in two consecutive nights, as I could not put it down once I started the book. <br/><br/>I was originally drawn to this book because although we have a wealth of novel/memoir written by Iranian women, I personally knew of none written by an Iranian man. I was curious to find out what a male perspective reflected in such format would contain, and I was not disappointed! Indeed, this is the first credit I would give the author. <br/><br/>I enjoyed the ease Seraji writes with; the vivid pictu</span>
  <span id="freeText4562517222089591150" style="display:none">I read Rooftops of Tehran in two consecutive nights, as I could not put it down once I started the book. <br /><br />I was originally drawn to this book because although we have a wealth of novel/memoir written by Iranian women, I personally knew of none written by an Iranian man. I was curious to find out what a male perspective reflected in such format would contain, and I was not disappointed! Indeed, this is the first credit I would give the author. <br /><br />I enjoyed the ease Seraji writes with; the vivid pictures of the ordinary life he depicts; and the richness of his characters. Ahmad is a central attraction among these characters and a hilariously funny and likable kid. Finally, I find a reflection on the seemingly long-gone reign of the Shah and his atrocities to be extremely timely at this turbulent time when the Iranian people seem to be back on the stage fighting for democracy. Iranians, especially the young generation, have suffered in hands of the Islamic regime so much that they tend to forget how oppressive the previous regime was, and even glorify that period “in comparison” with the IRI. It is very useful to be reminded how deep the flaws of the previous regime ran into the society. <br /><br />I sincerely hope I read more books by Mahbod Seraji. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4562517222089591150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9592867924226570771">What a beautiful, tender, tragic, comic, sweet and spellbinding book this is! <br/>Had someone described the circumstances of the "love story" beforehand, I might not have wanted to read it. Generally I can't stand love triangles, but the one in this coming of age novel is not a typical one nor is the love story stereotypical. <br/> In the author's own words this is a story about "friendship and humor, love and hope, universal experiences valued by people in all times and places". <br/>If you don't know about </span>
  <span id="freeText9592867924226570771" style="display:none">What a beautiful, tender, tragic, comic, sweet and spellbinding book this is! <br />Had someone described the circumstances of the "love story" beforehand, I might not have wanted to read it. Generally I can't stand love triangles, but the one in this coming of age novel is not a typical one nor is the love story stereotypical. <br /> In the author's own words this is a story about "friendship and humor, love and hope, universal experiences valued by people in all times and places". <br />If you don't know about Iran and its history, you will learn a lot. If you are already with Iran and its history you will appreciate it a lot more after reading this.<br />This is probably one of my favourite reads this year. It along with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4101648.Burnt_Shadows" title="Burnt Shadows by Kamila Shamsie" rel="nofollow noopener">Burnt Shadows</a> definitely moved me the most, although Rooftops of Tehran, like many novels where the lead character is a teen, has lighter moments balancing out the heavier parts and thus made it overall a more enjoyable read.<br />Yes, I recommend this book to everyone!</span>

    <a title="Michaiah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2493847-michaiah"><img alt="Michaiah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336440304p2/2493847.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/110940523?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michaiah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michaiah" href="/user/show/2493847-michaiah">Michaiah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2493847-michaiah?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer110940523&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer110940523" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5457802305370055860">I'm not sure why this book has received so many good reviews. The first part of the book held my interest fairly well, but about halfway through I just couldn't wait to get done, and actually skimmed much of the last 75 or so pages. It was ok I guess, but there was far too little about Iran and the historical situation at the time, and far too much irrelevant narration. For example, at one point, the narrator spends several pages describing how his aunt and uncle don't get along (but really do) </span>
  <span id="freeText5457802305370055860" style="display:none">I'm not sure why this book has received so many good reviews. The first part of the book held my interest fairly well, but about halfway through I just couldn't wait to get done, and actually skimmed much of the last 75 or so pages. It was ok I guess, but there was far too little about Iran and the historical situation at the time, and far too much irrelevant narration. For example, at one point, the narrator spends several pages describing how his aunt and uncle don't get along (but really do) when that has absolutely nothing to do with anything in the story at all. Seriously, nothing. I think it was supposed to be humorous, but it was actually just stupid. The story was extremely predictable, and yet I was left very confused about why certain things happened the way they did. For example, why did the secret police let Zari live, but insist that she and her family go into exile and represent to the rest of the world that she died when she set herself on fire?? That seemed totally unrealistic to me. The writing was not anything special, and I actually found it pretty awkward through most of the book. It was also just a little too cutesy and sappy for me, but I guess I should've expected that with main characters who are 17 years old. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5457802305370055860" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer12802788015929090075"> I've read three books recently : first Mother by Gorky, then Animal Farm and now this ; all set against a backdrop of capitalism,marxism & socialism ; all emphasizing one thing : if citizens are not politically aware and outspoken,they'll be exploited by those in power.This book was a strong portrayal of fear and helplessness in face of Shah's dictatorship in Iran in 1973-74.I liked the cultural comparisons between Iranians and Americans : Iranians are masters of subtle communication,they say t</span>
  <span id="freeText12802788015929090075" style="display:none"> I've read three books recently : first Mother by Gorky, then Animal Farm and now this ; all set against a backdrop of capitalism,marxism &amp; socialism ; all emphasizing one thing : if citizens are not politically aware and outspoken,they'll be exploited by those in power.This book was a strong portrayal of fear and helplessness in face of Shah's dictatorship in Iran in 1973-74.I liked the cultural comparisons between Iranians and Americans : Iranians are masters of subtle communication,they say things without words.Iranians openly lament at funerals while Americans try to show restraint but later end up in psychiatrist's couches.<br /><br /> I think Pasha was portrayed as a near-perfect Smart Alec which was annoying.Ahmad was really funny.The friendship of the four teenagers in an eastern setting as unrealistic.I agreed with the views of Pasha's otherwise radical school teacher Mr.Kermani,'I know you're one of those kids who enjoy sitting by the fire and reading philosophical bullshit instead of proven formulas that might save this nation from the grip of backwardness'.Higher education is the way to do something valuable for our third-world countries.But then I wonder if I'm like the horse, Boxer in Animal Farm.Boxer was remarkably hard-working but politically ignorant and thought that working hard was the answer to his every problem.He ended up dying pathetically.Political awareness and outspokenness is a must for everyone.<br /><br /> Character of Doctor reminded me of a radical class fellow who left the medical university in 1st year to go for jehad.Five years later,he was arrested for questioning from a mosque.Why people think jehad is only fighting with guns.He could have had a greater influence and chance of propagating his beliefs had he become a professional.Pity.Such a waste of talent.<br /><br /> Overall,a quick,easy and enjoyable read.<br /><br /> </span>

<div id="review_366987762" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/366987762" />
    <a title="Pari" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10067684-pari"><img alt="Pari" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1441934889p2/10067684.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/366987762?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pari" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pari" href="/user/show/10067684-pari">Pari</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer366987762" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15469491081119819329">Rooftops of Tehran started out to be promising and tragic, but ended flat and disappointing. <br/><br/>I loved the beginning of the book and immediately felt immersed in Iranian life. I feel like the characteristics of each character were exaggerated to an extreme degree, but it worked for this book. I've also never come across a novel that deals with the oppression and fear of pre-revolution Iran during the Shah's reign (as opposed to the many that talk about post-revolution Iran and Khomeini). It was re</span>
  <span id="freeText15469491081119819329" style="display:none">Rooftops of Tehran started out to be promising and tragic, but ended flat and disappointing. <br /><br />I loved the beginning of the book and immediately felt immersed in Iranian life. I feel like the characteristics of each character were exaggerated to an extreme degree, but it worked for this book. I've also never come across a novel that deals with the oppression and fear of pre-revolution Iran during the Shah's reign (as opposed to the many that talk about post-revolution Iran and Khomeini). It was refreshing to read something about Iran that took place right at the cusp of a volatile political time, but whose main character wasn't particularly political to begin with.<br /><br />The problem for me came in about the last ten pages. Without giving it away, the twist was unexpected, but I was so disappointed in it. I feel like the ending is probably a make-or-break point for many readers; they'll either love it and love the book even more, or dislike it and then end the book with a bitter taste. Unfortunately I was of the latter camp.<br /><br />Overall, I think I would recommend this book because of its ability to take a reader to pre-revolution Iran; it begins to set the stage for the revolution. You get a peek at some of the discontent boiling in Iran and - if you didn't already - begin to better understand the desire for change. <br /><br />When/if you read Rooftops of Tehran, maybe just stop reading ten pages before the real end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15469491081119819329" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/68566282?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily" href="/user/show/148902-emily">Emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">pretty much anyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/148902-emily?shelf=alltimefavorites">alltimefavorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/148902-emily?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer68566282" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12296309605564907074">Rooftops of Tehran is so many things: semi-autobiographical, a beautifully done coming of age story, a portrait of a specific time and place (Tehran 1973-74). It's peopled with wonderful characters, the kind who stay with you after you've finished the book, who pop into your head for a visit like old friends. It's also one of the best portraits of teenage life I've ever read. The way Seraji evokes Tehran and the Persian culture, the smells and tastes, sights and sounds is so powerful that I felt</span>
  <span id="freeText12296309605564907074" style="display:none">Rooftops of Tehran is so many things: semi-autobiographical, a beautifully done coming of age story, a portrait of a specific time and place (Tehran 1973-74). It's peopled with wonderful characters, the kind who stay with you after you've finished the book, who pop into your head for a visit like old friends. It's also one of the best portraits of teenage life I've ever read. The way Seraji evokes Tehran and the Persian culture, the smells and tastes, sights and sounds is so powerful that I felt like I was there. I could hear it, see it, taste it, smell it. Perhaps my favorite thing about the book is that it was at once so familiar and yet so foreign, in a way that felt totally organic. From the first page, you identify with the narrator Pasha. The novel pulls you in and doesn't let you go until the very last page. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12296309605564907074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1364280220" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1364280220" />
    <a title="Abbie Olson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15825512-abbie-olson"><img alt="Abbie Olson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512571109p2/15825512.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abbie Olson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abbie Olson" href="/user/show/15825512-abbie-olson">Abbie Olson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1364280220" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5409958829476302312">This is one of those rare books that takes your breath away. The history of political unrest in 1970s Iran is fascinating enough, but throw in beautiful prose and a coming-of-age story, and you get a masterpiece. Lovely, heart-wrenching, and hilarious as hell, this book will sweep you away. When you let it steal your heart, you'll have a helluva time finding your way back to reality.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2878777056?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Snigdha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Snigdha" href="/user/show/22247463-snigdha">Snigdha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22247463-snigdha?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22247463-snigdha?shelf=romance">romance</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2878777056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2378151541664481087"><b>“Loving is a laborious and complex business.” : 2.4/5 </b><br/><br/>I picked up this book thinking it will be the same as all those books set in middle eastern backdrop, and it will be more focused upon the culture and their people but it is a plain, humdrum love story which has nothing great to offer from a reader's perspective. <br/><br/>This book is set in 1970's Iran, where a teenage guy falls in love with his mentor's girlfriend, who is elder to him as well, at first he tries to ward off his feelings for her but w</span>
  <span id="freeText2378151541664481087" style="display:none"><b>“Loving is a laborious and complex business.” : 2.4/5 </b><br /><br />I picked up this book thinking it will be the same as all those books set in middle eastern backdrop, and it will be more focused upon the culture and their people but it is a plain, humdrum love story which has nothing great to offer from a reader's perspective. <br /><br />This book is set in 1970's Iran, where a teenage guy falls in love with his mentor's girlfriend, who is elder to him as well, at first he tries to ward off his feelings for her but with the turn of events he finds himself helplessly falling for her and be love lorn till the climax of the book.<br /><br />The writing is very much plain, agreed and basic, many of the places you feel Deja vu like you have read and heard these lines a million times before, also many lines are repetitive in the book. The plot is very predictable and expected, there is only one high point in the whole book, but soon you guess what will happen next. Author could have included many romantic verses and interesting character build up, but he choose to write a mundane story which seemed to be told for millionth time. <br /><br />Overall it's a very average romance novel, which even left unread will cause no pain.</span>
